Rule 3 - Thinking Process
================================================================================
Okay, let's analyze the given OpenHABy rules to see if they exhibit any of the identified threat patterns. 

First, I'll review the two rules provided.

Rule 1: 
- Trigger: Item Luz_gTER_SalaEstar_Hall changes.
- Action: sendCommand on Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall to set its state to the current state of Luz_gTER_SalaEstar_Hall.

Rule 2: 
- Trigger: Item Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall changes.
- Action: sendCommand on Luz_gTER_SalaEstar_Hall to set its state to the current state of Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall.

Now, let's check each threat pattern:

1. **Action Contradiction (AC):** This occurs when two rules have conflicting actions affecting the same item with opposite effects.
   - Both rules are targeting Luz_gTER_SalaEstar_Hall and Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall. Rule 1 is sending a command based on Luz_gTER_SalaEstar_Hall's state, and Rule 2 does the same for Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall. There's no direct contradiction in actions since each affects a different item. So, AC doesn't apply here.

Wait, but maybe I misread. Wait: Both rules are sending commands related to two bulbs (Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall and Luz_gTER_SalaEstar_Hall). Let me clarify:
Rule 1 says when Bulb A changes state, Command B is sent to Bulb B setting it to Bulb A's state.
Rule 2 says when Bulb B changes state, Command C is sent to Bulb A setting it from Bulb B.

So no direct contradiction in actions on the same bulb. So AC isn't triggered here.

2. **Triggers Cascade (TC):** This happens when one rule's action triggers another rule.
   - Rule 1 sets a command for Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall based on Luz_gTER_SalaEstar_Hall changes.
   - Then Rule 2 checks if Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall has changed. So, if both have changed independently, it's possible that after performing Rule 1, the state of Bulb B might match in a way that triggers Rule 2 again.

But I'm not sure this creates an intentional cause-effect chain because each rule is based on independent changes. Wait: If both bulbs change at different times or under certain conditions, could their trigger actions interfere?

Wait, Rule 1's action affects Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall's state to match Luz_gTER_SalaEstar_Hall. Then, if Luz_gTER_Brinquedo_Hall changes again due to another rule, would that cause Rule 2 to fire? Or maybe when the trigger conditions are independent, it could lead to multiple triggers without a causal link.

I'm not sure this fits TC exactly because both are separate actions based on different items. So perhaps TC isn't triggered here either.

3. **Condition Cascade (CC):** This is about one rule's action enabling another condition.
   - Both rules have their own conditions, but neither directly depends on the other unless they influence each other via shared variables. However, in this case, Rule 1 sets a state of Bulb B to match Bulb A, and Rule 2 looks at Bulb B independently.

Wait, Rule 1's action might affect Rule 2's condition indirectly because after sending a command on Bulb B based on Bulb A, if Bulb B changes again through another means, it could trigger Rule 2. But this would be more of an unintended effect rather than Condition Cascade as per the definition.

But according to the definitions, CC requires that one rule's action sets up a condition in another.

In our case, after executing Rule1 (which directly affects Bulb B), if Bulb B is already set based on Bulb A and it also changes, then it might trigger Rule2. But I'm not sure if this counts as setting conditions because the triggers are based on items themselves changing, not relying on rules.

Alternatively, maybe when both rules detect a change in different bulbs, their actions (setting each other's states) could cause a loop: Bulb B sets to Bulb A's state, then if Bulb B changes again, it sets Bulb A back. But this might be more of an endless loop of triggers rather than CC.

I think none of the conditions are set up so that Rule1 affects Rule2's condition directly. So CC isn't triggered here either.

So, after reviewing all possibilities, I don't see any of the defined threat patterns in these rules.

- **Condition Cascade (CC):** The first rule's condition is based on the state of nScene_FF_Lights changing to 0. If that's true, it sends commands. However, nothing directly uses another rule's output as a condition here because each rule relies on its own trigger and conditions are separate.

Rule 7 - Thinking Process
================================================================================
Okay, I'm trying to analyze whether the given OpenHAB rules have any Rule Interaction Threats (RITs) based on the predefined threat classifications: Action Contradiction (AC), Trigger Cascade (TC), and Condition Cascade (CC).

First, let me look at the two rules provided.

The first rule is "First floor Scene 9 - full alarm". Its trigger is when an item nScene_FF_Lights receives a command of 9. The then part sends several commands to turn on various lights in different rooms on the first floor. So, whenever that light sensor triggers, it sends multiple sendCommand actions.

The second rule is "FF Actuator 1". It's triggered when sLight_FirstFl_Act01 changes state. That can be either ON or OFF. Depending on whether the state is ON or OFF, it will send a command to turn the corridor light ON or OFF.

Now, let me check for each threat:

Action Contradiction (AC): This occurs when two rules affect the same item with conflicting actions. Looking at both rules, in the first rule, multiple lights are turned on, and the second rule affects sLight_FirstFl_Act01 directly by turning it ON or OFF based on its state change.

Do these triggers cause a conflict? Well, if nScene_FF_Lights receives command 9, which presumably turns on all the corridor lights. Then, if any of those corridor lights (like sLight_FirstFl_Acorn01) change state, their actuator could turn it off or on again. But in the first rule's then part, after sending commands to turn them ON, there's no action that would send a command back to turn them OFF based on subsequent changes.

However, the second rule is directly triggered when any corridor light (sLight_FirstFl_Act01) changes. So if the first rule turns all corridor lights ON, and one of them becomes OFF later, the second rule will detect this change in state and send a command accordingly.

But does this cause an action contradiction? Since sendCommand(sLightmailer_*) is called multiple times with various actions (ON/OFF). However, as long as each individual action's condition is correctly managed by triggers, and there's no direct conflict between the rules' actions on the same item in conflicting directions without a condition-based override.

In this case, if nScene_FF_Lights is already triggered to send ON commands, then subsequent state changes might not necessarily overlap with contradictions because they are part of different triggers. Therefore, maybe no AC here.

Next, Trigger Cascade (TC): This happens when the action of one rule triggers another rule's execution. So in this case, the first rule's trigger is when an item receives command 9, and it then triggers sendCommands for multiple lights. None of these actions directly change any other switch or trigger items that could cause a cascade because each corridor light acts independently.

In the second rule, when sLight_FirstFl_Act01 changes state, it uses its own actuator to turn itself ON or OFF without relying on anything else. So I don't see an action in one rule triggering another's condition.

Finally, Condition Cascade (CC): This is when actions set conditions for other rules. In the first rule, none of the actions have any conditions beyond the automatic sendCommand. The second rule uses item.state == value, which doesn't necessarily depend on previous conditions because each condition is evaluated based on its own triggers and state.

But wait, in the second rule's then block, there are two conditions: if (sLight_FirstFl_Act01.state == ON) and if (extremeWarning.state == OFF). The first depends on sLight's state, which was set by the first rule. So if multiple corridor lights were turned ON from different triggers, could that cause an unintended condition overlap?

But in each then block of a trigger rule, each action is part of the same when clause. However, since each sendCommand command sends a single action (ON or OFF), as long as they are handled correctly within their own conditions based on their specific triggers.

I think the main issue here could be with multiple actions setting different states without an overlap in triggered orders that might cause unintended behavior, but perhaps not necessarily a full trigger cascade. So I'm leaning towards no TC or CC threats.

Putting it all together: The rules don't have conflicting actions, triggers don't cascade each other's triggers, and there are no condition cascades beyond what the second rule is already handling.

Alright, I'm analyzing two OpenHAh rules to see if they fit any of the defined threat patterns. Let's go through each step.

First, looking at Rule 1: "First floor Scene 0 - lights off." It triggers when an item (nScene_FF_Lights) receives a command '0'. The action is turning several corridors and study-related lights off. So this rule turns off multiple lights upon receiving a command.

Rule 2 is "CR Actuator 1." It's triggered when the sLight_KidsRoom_Act01 changes state. In its then block, it checks if the state is ON or OFF and sends commands accordingly. Specifically, if the actuator is pressed (maybe another sensor), it either turns on Light 02 or off.

Now, checking threats:

AC: Action Contradiction. This happens when two rules affect the same item with opposite actions. Looking at Rule1, after triggering, multiple lights are turned OFF. But in this case, each trigger deals with different items. Rule1 is about nScene_FF_Lights command '0', while Rule2 triggers on sLight_KidsRoom_Act01. So no direct conflicting actions here.

TC: Trigger Cascade. This occurs when one rule's action triggers another rule. Here, Rule1 turns off lights that are then manipulated by Rule2 based on their state. For example, if after turning off the study lights (Rule1), Rule2 checks sLight_Study and might change it again. That seems like a trigger cascade because the action in Rule1 affects Rule2's execution.

CC: Condition Cascade is when an action sets a variable used in another condition. In this case, after turning off the lights, Rule2 doesn't directly rely on that state for its conditions beyond checking sLight_KidsRoom_Act01. There's no shared variable setup; it's more about trigger order affecting execution.

But TC seems applicable because when nScene_FF_Lights is triggered to '0', Rule1 runs and sets various lights OFF, which might then cause a subsequent check in another rule (like when an actuator activates), causing Rule2 to change state again. This could lead to unintended behavior if the sequence isn't controlled.

So the main threat here appears to be Trigger Cascade.

Okay, so I'm trying to analyze these two OpenHA rules and see if they fit any of the defined threats: AC for Action Contradiction, TC for Trigger Cascade, or possibly CC for Condition Cascade. Let me go through each step carefully.

First, let's look at the structure of each rule. 

The first rule is "r2 - ModeChange". It triggers when the item OPS_MODE receives an update. Inside this rule, there are multiple conditions based on the state of OPS_MODE. For example, if OPS_MODE is closed (state 0), it logs and sends commands to turn RELAY_1 and RELAY_2 off. If it's in auto mode (state 1), it turns them on starting at 8 AM until sunset, then off after sunset. And if it's open (state 2), it turns both relays on.

The second rule is "r4 - SunsetAction". It triggers when the item SUNSET_EVENT changes to ON. Then, if OPS_MODE is in auto mode (state 1), it sends commands to turn RELAY_1 and RELAY_2 off.

Now, let's check for threats.

1. Action Contradiction: This happens when two rules cause conflicting actions on the same item. In this case, both r2 and r4 send commands to RELAY_1 and RELAY_2. R2 turns them off under certain conditions (state 0 or during specific times) and r4 also turns them off when SUNSET_EVENT is triggered with OPS_MODE at state 1. It doesn't seem like the actions are conflicting; instead, they might be complementary in some cases but not necessarily contradictory.

Wait a minute, actually in r2's case under auto mode (state 1), it turns relays on from 8 AM to sunset and off after. The r4 rule is triggered whenever the SUNSET_EVENT occurs ON, specifically when OPS_MODE is state 1, which could overlap with certain times. So if both are active at the same time, they would indeed send conflicting commands for relay 1 and 2—sending ON in r2's auto mode while also sending OFF via r4. This seems like a possible AC threat.

2. Trigger Cascade: This happens when one rule's action triggers another rule's trigger condition. For example, if turning on the relays in r2 might cause another rule to execute. But looking at these rules, r2 uses sendCommand to set states or conditions that are within the same rule. There's no external rule involved here that would be triggered by these actions, so I don't think this applies.

3. Condition Cascade: This occurs when one rule's action sets a condition in another rule. Again, reviewing these rules, r2 has multiple if conditions based on OPS_MODE state, but they are all within the same rule. There's no external variable that changes and then triggers another rule through its conditions. So this doesn't seem to apply either.

However, I think there might be a possible overlap with Condition Cascade in some cases because when OPS_MODE is set to 1 (auto), r2 sets relays on until sunset, which could indirectly affect other rules involving the relays or operations mode. But since these rules are standalone and don't reference external conditions beyond what's defined within them, it's not a clear case of Condition Cascade.

So, considering all that, the primary threat seems to be Action Contradiction between r2 and r4 when both trigger actions on the same relays in conflicting ways under certain conditions. That would definitely cause unintended behavior if all three relays are involved.

Rule 65 - Thinking Process
================================================================================
Okay, I need to analyze the given OpenHAB rules for potential Rule Interaction Threats (RITs) based on the defined threat classifications. Let's go through each step carefully.

   - Trigger: When `Item sSunrise_Event` changes to ON.
   - Actions: 
     - postUpdate(sDay, ON)
     - postUpdate(sNight, OFF)

2. **Nighttime**:
   - Trigger: When `Item sSunset.Event` changes to ON.
   - Actions:
     - postUpdate(sDay, OFF)
     - postUpdate(sNight, ON)

Now, I'll check each threat classification one by one.

### Action Contrast (AC):
This occurs when two rules conflict in actions and share overlapping triggers. Here, both rules are separate; Daytime is triggered on sunrise, Nighttime on sunset. They don't overlap in terms of their triggers because they're designed for opposite times. The actions they perform seem to be inverses but do not directly conflict with each other. For example, when Daytime runs, it turns sDay ON and sNight OFF; Nighttime does the opposite. Since one is about daytime and the other nighttime, they don't interfere with each other's conditions. So, AC doesn't apply here.

### Trigger Cascade (TC):
This happens when an action in one rule triggers another rule. Looking at Daytime's actions: it sets sDay ON and sNight OFF. Do these actions match any triggers of other rules? In this case, Nighttime is about the sunset event, which is separate from the state changes of sDay or sNight. Similarly, Nighttime's actions turn sDay OFF and sNight ON—but again, their trigger is a different event (sunset). There’s no rule here that has an action matching another rule's trigger command. Thus, TC doesn't apply.

### Condition Cascade (CC):
This occurs when the condition in one rule depends on an action from another rule. Let's see if any rule's action affects another rule's condition indirectly. For example, Daytime sets sDay to ON and sNight to OFF. Do these affect the conditions of Nighttime? Nighttime looks for sSunset.Event changing to ON but does not reference sDay or sNight states elsewhere unless there are other rules that depend on them. Similarly, Nighttime's actions trigger based on sunset, which doesn't directly influence Daytime's trigger condition beyond their mutual dependency on time events. 

Wait a minute—are the triggers dependent on each other? Because sSunrise.Event occurs at sunrise and sSunset.Event at sunset. In many household scenarios, these are mutually exclusive events; lighting shouldn't be active during both. However, based solely on the given rules, when sunrise happens (Daytime rule activates), it sets sDay to ON and sNight to OFF. Conversely, at sunset, Nighttime turns sDay OFF and sNight ON. Nowhere in the conditions do these actions set or clear other things beyond the direct variables involved.

But perhaps looking deeper: The trigger for Daytime is based on an item (sSunrise_Event) changing to ON, and for Nighttime, it's another item (sSunset.Event) changing to ON. These triggers are independent unless there's a setup where both could be triggered simultaneously or interfere with each other in a way that their actions affect the triggers of one another.

Alternatively, is any rule's action affecting another rule's condition? For example, if sDay is supposed to stay lit during the day and off at night. But the rules here directly set these variables on trigger events, which are based on sunrise and sunset—seemingly independent conditions unless they can't happen at the same time.

In this setup, the triggers are separate (sunrise vs. sunset), so their actions shouldn't interfere with each other's triggers because they're supposed to be triggered sequentially or mutually exclusively.

Are there any indirect dependencies? For instance, if sDay is being turned ON by Daytime and then used in another rule downstream, that could create a CC threat. However, with the given rules, there are no subsequent rules referencing sDay after its action, so it doesn't chain further.

Therefore, I don't see evidence of Condition Cascade here either.

### Conclusion:
Looking at all three threats—AC, TC, and CC—none seem to apply because the triggers are independent (sunrise vs. sunset), actions set specific variables without mutual dependency beyond their intended timing, and conditions aren't influenced by other rules' actions downstream in a cascading manner.

Alright, I need to analyze these two OpenHAB rules for potential Rule Interaction Threats (RITs). Let's go through each step carefully.

First, let's look at what RITs are being looked for here. The task is to check if there are any of the three mentioned threats: Action Contradiction (AC), Trigger Cascade (TC), or Condition Cascade (CC).

Looking at the first rule, "Activate fire alarm":
- Triggers when cAlarmActivation_Fire changes to OPEN.
- Actions include setting presence status to FIRE, logging an alarm, and activating sirens and strobes by sending commands.

The second rule, "Alarm sirens mode 1 - sirene setting":
- Triggers when nSirens_GeneralMode receives a command of 1.
- Action is setting Siren_01_Mode to 0 (OFF) and then general mode to OFF as well.

Now, let's check each threat:

Action Contradiction (AC):
This occurs when two rules with opposite actions affect the same item. For example, one rule turning aLight on while another turns it off.
In this case, both rules involve sending commands related to sirens andgeneral mode setting nSirens_GeneralMode. The second rule sends the command to turn the siren mode to OFF (SIRENSOFF). Wait, is SIRENSOFF equivalent to 0? Yes, because command values for switches are often numerical with ON/OFF as binary.
So, if "nSirens_GeneralMode" was previously set by the first rule when fire alarm activated, the second rule might be trying to turn it off. This could cause an unintended reset of the alarm's siren mode.

Trigger Cascade (TC):
This happens when one rule triggers another's execution. For example, Rule1's action sets a value, which is then used as part of Rule2's trigger.
In our case, the first rule sends "sendCommand(nSirens_GeneralMode, FIREALARM)", but looking at the variable name, nSirens_GeneralMode, it might be expecting an integer. However, in the second rule, when triggered by receiving a command 1, it sets Siren_01_Mode to 0 (OFF) and general mode to OFF as well.
Wait, does the first rule's action set nSirens_GeneralMode to FIREALARM, which is likely treated as an integer, say 1? Or is FIREALARM a command for that specific siren unit?
Alternatively, maybe the trigger in the second rule (Command 1) leads it to run independently but affects the same variable. But I'm not sure if the trigger condition directly matches another rule's effect.

Condition Cascade (CC):
This occurs when an action sets a value used in another rule's condition.
In this case, maybe the first rule doesn't have any conditions beyond the initial trigger, but it activates several actions regardless of any subsequent variables. The second rule triggers based on command 1 received by nSirens_GeneralMode.

Putting it together: When fire alarm is activated (first rule), the general mode is set to SIRENSOFF (0). Alternatively, perhaps both rules are setting the same variable in different ways.

Rule 1 is for the Garden Door, and Rule 2 is for the Kitchen Door. Both have their own blocks that run when the corresponding sensor opens.

Now, according to the threat definitions:

1. **Action Contradiction (AC):** This occurs when two rules conflict in actions affecting the same item with opposite commands. For example, one rule opening a door while another tries to close it. But here, both rules are activating similar actions like logging and updating statuses when their respective doors open. So there's no direct action contradiction.

2. **Trigger Cascade (TC):** This happens if one rule's action triggers another rule. In these rules, the triggers depend on sensor changes, but since each door has its own trigger, opening the garden door doesn't affect whether the kitchen door opens or not. Also, neither rule uses an action to directly call another rule's condition.

3. **Condition Cascade (CC):** This would happen if one rule's action enables another rule's condition. Looking at the rules, both actions are logging and status updates without any conditions that depend on other rules' outcomes. Each rule acts independently based on their own sensor state.

I don't see any overlap in triggers except for being triggered by similar sensors but on different items (Garden vs. Kitchen). The rules aren't affecting each other's items, so no trigger cascade between them. Also, the actions themselves, like logging messages and updating statuses, don't interfere with each other or create conflicts because they're separate doors.

Therefore, these two rules don't present any of the three threats mentioned: AC, TC, or CC.

First, let's understand what each threat entails. 

1. **AC - Action Contradiction**: This happens when two rules conflict with each other's actions on the same item, directly opposing the desired outcomes.
2. **TC - Trigger Cascade**: When one rule's action triggers another rule's execution, possibly unintentionally and causing unintended consequences.
3. **CC - Condition Cascade**: An action from one rule sets a condition in another rule that might be triggered without cause if actions overlap or conditions are not properly checked.

Now, looking at the given rules:

Rule 1:
- Trigger: Item Luz_gSUP_Casal_Escritorio1 changes

- Trigger: Item Luz_gSUP_Casal_Escritorio2 changes

Similar logic here: when Luz 2 changes state, Luz 1 is told to change.

```plaintext

when 
    Item phone_Josh received update OFF
then 
    postUpdate(josh, "Away")
end
```

Then, Condition Cascade (CC): This is when a rule's action sets up another condition in another rule. In Rule 3, the actions are setting Josh's mode to Home or Away based on phone_Josh states. However, I don't see any other rules that check josh's state after that. So unless there's an else clause using another rule, CC isn't triggered here.

But I don't see a direct condition cascade here because there's no rule where Joshua's state triggers further action. Therefore, CC isn't applicable either.

So putting it all together: Neither AC, TC, nor CC are present in these rules.

Rule 289 - Thinking Process
================================================================================
Alright, I need to analyze the given OpenHAB rules and determine if they exhibit any of the threat patterns listed: AC (Action Contradiction) or TC (Trigger Cascade). Let's break down each rule and see how they might interact.

First, looking at Rule 3 - "System Start":
- When the system starts, it checks phone_Josh.state.
- If phone_Josh.state is ON, it sets josh to "Home".
- Else if phone_Josh_WORK.state is ON, it sets josh to "Work".
- Else (neither is ON), it sets josh to "Away".

So this rule uses three conditions based on the state of two variables: phone_Josh and phone_Josh_WORK.

Next, Rule 5 - "Josh leaves home":
- The trigger is when Item phone_Josh received an update set to OFF.
- Then, regardless of anything else, it sends a command to josh setting it to "Away".

Now, evaluating if these rules have any threats:

1. **Action Contradiction (AC):**
   - AC occurs when two rules conflict in their actions with overlapping triggers.
   - In this case, Rule 3's actions are about setting josh to "Home", "Work", or "Away".
   - Rule 5 sets josh to "Away" regardless of any conditions.
   - The action in Rule 5 (Setting to Away) might conflict with the else clause in Rule 3 where if neither phone_Josh nor phone_Josh_WORK is ON, it also sets to Away. There's no direct contradiction here because both actions are setting josh to Away but under different contexts.
   - However, since Rule 5 is a simple action without any conditions (it just triggers on phone_Josh being OFF), any situation where Rule 3 applies could be affected. If someone uses an item that turns phone_Josh OFF after the system started, they might get josh set to Away regardless of the previous state in Rule 3.
   - But AC requires conflicting actions with overlapping triggers. Here, the trigger for Rule 5 is when phone_Josh received update OFF, while Rule 3's trigger depends on the current state. They don't have overlapping triggers because one is a command (OFF) and the other is checking if it's ON or not.
   - Therefore, there might be no direct AC.

2. **Trigger Cascade (TC):**
   - TC occurs when one rule's action triggers another rule's execution.
   - Looking at Rule 5's trigger: if phone_Josh received an update OFF, then sendUpdate(josh, Away).
   - In Rule 3, the action depends on phone_Josh's state. If, after Rule 5 runs (with josh set to Away), phone_Josh's state changes in a way that affects other conditionals.
   - However, since Rule 5 is only a direct command based on an update, unless there are dependencies elsewhere, it's not clear if Rule 3 would be triggered again as a result of Rule 5. Alternatively, if the 'System started' event could trigger both Rules 3 and others but there isn't overlap here.
   - Another angle: when phone_JoshORK, does turning itself OFF (triggering Rule 5) cause any other action that might lead to another rule being executed unintentionally? But in this setup, it seems isolated.

Considering these points, neither AC nor TC appears directly linked between the two rules as presented. They handle different triggers and their actions don't create direct contradictions or cascades.

Rule 295 - Thinking Process
================================================================================
Alright, let's analyze the provided OpenHAB rules to see if they fall into any of the identified threat categories: Action Contradiction (AC), Trigger Cascade (TC), or Condition Cascade (CC).

The given rules are part of a system called Timer Demo in OpenHAB. The rule handles turning on and off Light_GF_Corridor_Ceiling based on a timer configuration, which is triggered when an item receives certain commands.

Looking at the structure:
1. When an item (Light_GF_Corridor_Ceiling) receives a command ("Item Light_GF_Corridor_Ceiling received command"), it sets up a timer to turn the light off after 10 seconds upon receiving an ON command, or clears any existing timer when turned OFF.

First, I check for AC. Are there conflicting actions on the same item? The rule doesn't send conflicting commands; it either starts a timer (ON) or stops it (OFF). No action contradictions here.

Next, looking for TC: Does one rule's action trigger another rule? The timer logic itself isn't triggered by another action in this rule. It seems isolated to handling the received command and timer setup/stop.

Lastly, CC: Does one rule activate conditions in another based on actions or previous state changes? Again, the rule handles its specific case without relying on other rules' states beyond the timer and light status itself. There are no dependencies that cause cascading conditions.

So none of the threats apply here; the rules are self-contained for their respective actions.
